Chapter 1

Chapter 1

Kiyoshi’s final letter arrived thirty years after the war ended, creased and smelling of brine. I held the future he’d predicted—our gleaming, sterile city—and saw only the past in his fading ink. He wrote of ghosts, and of me. I am the ghost.



“For you, Zixin. A ghost came.”

The envelope was thick, yellowed at the edges. The address was not printed, but written in a hand I hadn’t seen in fifteen years—a script that carved characters with the sharp, unforgiving angles of a bamboo stalk breaking. My grandmother’s hand. The postmark was a smudge, but the stamp was clear: not the digital watermark of the Republic, but the old, embossed dragon of the pre-Collapse Imperial Mail. It was impossible. She was dead. She had been dead for a decade, her ashes scattered from the Peak into the South China Sea.

I paid Fai for a pack of clove cigarettes I didn’t want. His eyes, milky with data-cataracts, didn’t leave my face. “Smells like old soil,” he said, then turned back to his flickering screens, the newsfeeds scrolling in endless, silent lament.

I walked. The city was a symphony of forgetting. Above, the new towers of chrome and photovoltaic glass sang their quiet, efficient song. At street level, in the canyon-bottoms where the sun never fully reached, the old world persisted like a stubborn mold. The smell of star anise and sewage, the clatter of mahjong tiles from a shuttered upper window, the sticky-sweet vapor of bubble tea carts. I moved through it, the letter a hot coal in the inner pocket of my jacket. I was a curator of the Museum of Fragmented Histories, a junior archivist tasked with sorting the digital detritus of the Collapse. My job was to authenticate ghosts, to give them proper labels and store them in climate-controlled servers. This ghost had bypassed the system entirely.

My apartment was a single cube in a stacked complex, a module that hummed with recycled air. I placed the envelope on the steel table. I made tea, the ritual of warming the pot, of watching the oolong leaves unfurl. I lit one of Fai’s clove cigarettes, the smoke a spicy, foreign plume in the sterile air. Then, with a bone knife I used for opening packages, I slit the top.

The paper inside was brittle, the texture of a dried leaf. It was not one letter, but two. The first was a single sheet.

Zixin,

If you are reading this, the first bird has flown. Do not trust the water from the old pipes. Do not speak of this to your mother. She chose her forgetting, and we must honor it. You, with your hands in the digital graves, you have the sickness of remembering. It is both your burden and your tool.

The second letter is not from me. It was entrusted to me, long ago, to be passed on when the season turned. I am only the hinge. It is written in a language you will think you do not know. You will be wrong. Read it with your skin, not your eyes. The past is not behind us. It is below. It is the foundation upon which this fragile city trembles.

There is a place. The Teahouse of the Drowned Moon. Ask for the window that faces the mountain. Sit there. Wait. Do not bring your devices.

—Nǎinai

My hands were steady, but a cold wire had been threaded down my spine. The sickness of remembering. She had said that to me when I was a child, when I would ask about the photographs she kept hidden, the ones with the faces scratched out. My mother called it superstition, the ramblings of an old woman scorched by history. We moved into a new-build zone, where the history was pre-installed, a bland, consensus narrative in pastel colors. We chose our forgetting.

The second letter was different. The paper was thicker, almost vellum. The script was not Chinese characters, nor any alphabet I recognized. It was a cascade of elegant, interlocking loops and sharp terminals, like waves crashing against rocks. It looked like Arabic, maybe, or an old Persian script, but distorted, pulled into shapes that felt… familiar. A visual echo. I ran a fingertip over the ink. It was raised slightly, as if drawn with a heavy hand. And there, in the bottom corner, a seal: not a stamp, but a hand-drawn impression of a lotus, inside which was a tiny, perfect key.

I read my grandmother’s note again. The Teahouse of the Drowned Moon. I knew it. Every historian of the old port district did. It was a relic, a wooden structure that had somehow survived the great fires of the Transition, tucked into a crooked alley behind the wholesale jade market. It was said to be a place for unsanctioned negotiations, for whispers that couldn’t survive the filtered air of the official zones. A place for ghosts.

I almost didn’t go. I am a rational woman. I work with verified data-streams and forensic palimpsests. This was paper and prophecy. This was the world of my grandmother, a world of omens and silent understandings, a world that had, according to the state archives, been neatly catalogued and filed away under “Outmoded Cultural Practices.”

But the hook was in me. The sickness of remembering. It was true. I spent my days listening to the whispers in the static of corrupted files, trying to piece together stories from the blacked-out sections of old manifests, from the ghost-images left in decaying surveillance hard drives. I was an archaeologist of the recently erased. This letter was an artifact that had walked, of its own accord, into my present.

I changed into plain, dark clothes. I left my tablet, my comms bracelet, my location tag on the table. Their absence felt like missing limbs. I took only the letters, my keys, and a handful of old Republic cash.

The walk to the port district was a descent through layers of time. The clean, wide boulevards gave way to narrower streets, where buildings leaned toward each other, sharing the weight of centuries. Neon signs in traditional script bled their colored light onto wet pavement. The air grew thicker, warmer, carrying the salt-tang of the harbor and the pungent scent of dried seafood. I felt watched, not by cameras—there were fewer here—but by the darkened windows, by the old women sorting greens on plastic stools, their eyes flicking over me, placing me as an outsider from the glass towers.

The alley leading to the Teahouse of the Drowned Moon was so narrow I could touch both walls with my elbows. It was marked only by a wooden plaque, the characters for “Moon” half-obscured by moss. The door was black lacquer, worn to grey in places. I pushed it open.

Sound died. The cacophony of the market faded into a dense, quiet hum. The air was warm and fragrant with the smell of aged pu’erh, sandalwood, and the faint, sweet decay of wet wood. The light was low, coming from paper lanterns that cast a soft, yolk-yellow glow. The room was a labyrinth of dark timber booths and faded silk partitions. It was crowded, yet utterly silent. Men and women, mostly old, sat alone or in pairs, drinking from tiny cups. No one spoke above a murmur. The only loud sounds were the click of porcelain on wood, the gentle hiss of a copper kettle over charcoal, the occasional, dry cough.

A woman materialized at my side. She was perhaps sixty, her hair a severe grey knot, her face a map of fine lines. She wore a high-collared jacket of dark blue hemp.

“You are in the wrong place,” she said, not as a question, but a statement. Her voice was the sound of two stones grinding softly together.

“I was told to ask for the window that faces the mountain.”

Her eyes, black and depthless, scanned my face. They paused at my temples, where the faint, pale lines of childhood eczema were barely visible. A mark she seemed to recognize. She gave a minute nod.

“Follow.”

She led me through the maze of booths, past drinkers who did not look up. At the very back of the teahouse was a small alcove, separated by a beaded curtain of polished river stones. Inside was a single, low table and a bench seat beneath a window. But the window did not face a mountain. It was a painting of a mountain—an ancient, vertical scroll depicting a mist-wreathed peak, done in ink wash on silk, so realistic it seemed a portal.

“Sit,” the woman said. “The mountain is not a geography. It is a memory. Face it and remember.”

She left. I sat on the hard bench, my back to the quiet room. I stared at the painted mountain. It was Lushan, I was sure. A place of poets and exiles. The brushstrokes were confident, lonely. Minutes ticked by, measured by the slow drip of condensation from a clay pot somewhere behind me. I felt foolish. This was a charade, a game for nostalgic fools.

Then, a shadow fell across the painting. A man slid into the booth opposite me. I had not heard him approach. He was older than me, perhaps in his late forties, with a weary, intelligent face. His hair was cropped short, peppered with grey. He wore the drab, functional clothes of a dockworker or a mechanic, but his hands were clean, the fingers long and precise.

He did not look at me. He looked at the mountain painting. When he spoke, his voice was low, meant only for the space between us.

“You have the letter.”

It was not a question. I said nothing. He finally turned his gaze on me. His eyes were a surprising light brown, like weak tea.

“Show me the seal.”

A thrill of danger, sharp and clean, cut through my confusion. How did he know about the seal? I hesitated, then reached into my inner pocket and withdrew the second letter. I placed it on the table, my finger covering the lotus-key seal. He leaned forward slightly, looked, and leaned back. A confirmation.

“Your grandmother was a brave woman. A terrible keeper of secrets, but brave.”

“She’s dead.”

“Yes. And yet her secrets are not. She was the last custodian of a correspondence that was never meant to be archived. A conversation across a rupture.”

“What conversation?”

He poured tea from a pot I hadn’t noticed into two cups I hadn’t seen. The gesture was automatic, graceful. He pushed one cup toward me.

“Between a prisoner and a warden. Between a past that refuses to die and a future that refuses to remember. Written in a language that was banned, then burned, then forgotten.”

I looked at the strange script on the letter. “What language is it?”

“It is called Nüshu,” he said. “The women’s script. A secret language created and used by women in a single river valley in Hunan, for centuries. A language for their eyes only. For letters, songs, laments. It was a whisper-network. It was almost eradicated. First by the Revolution, which saw it as feudal superstition. Then by the Great Homogenization. Then by the Collapse.” He took a sip of tea. “This,” he nodded at the letter, “is not just Nüshu. It is a variant. A coded version. Used by a specific… sisterhood.”

The word hung in the fragrant air. Sisterhood. It conjured images of my grandmother and her two silent friends, the way they would sit in the courtyard, their heads close together, writing in notebooks they would snap shut if I came near.

“Who are you?” I asked.

“My name is Leo. I was a friend to your grandmother. An apprentice in forgetting. Now, I am a librarian of lost things.” A faint, tired smile touched his lips. “Like you.”

“I work for the Museum.”

“You work for the Ministry of Memory. You sanitize ghosts. I try to listen to what they are saying before they are erased.”

“And what is this ghost saying?” I tapped the letter.

“I cannot read it. Not fully. Only a custodian, a daughter of the line, can truly decipher it. Your grandmother was one. You may be another, if the sickness is strong enough.”

“This is absurd. Secret languages, sisterhoods… this is pre-Collapse folklore.”

“Is it?” He leaned forward, his voice dropping even further. “Zixin, why do you think you were hired by the Museum? For your brilliant mind? There are a thousand brilliant minds. You were hired because your biometrics—your neural patterns, your epigenetic markers—show a high affinity for non-linear memory retention. You see patterns in the noise. You hear the echoes. They want to use that to find and neutralize historical… anomalies. They just don’t call them that. They call them ‘data-corruptions.’ Your grandmother knew this would happen. She knew the sickness would call to you, and that they would call to the sickness. This letter is a test. And a map.”

The painted mountain seemed to swirl before my eyes. I felt the ground of my certainties, my rational, catalogued world, crack and shift. “A map to what?”

“To the first bird,” Leo said. “The one that has flown. It is a metaphor. In the sisterhood’s script, it means a breach. A message that has gotten out. A secret that is now in motion.” He finished his tea. “The letter you hold is a reply. A reply to a message sent from a place that officially no longer exists. A place called ‘The Island of Unfinished Sentences.’ You need to find the letter to which this is the reply. Only then will the path forward—or backward—become clear.”

“Where is this island?”

“It is not on any map you can access. Its location is in the letter. Decipher it.” He stood up, his movement fluid and final. “They are already watching you. The delivery of the physical letter was an event. A blip. It will have been noticed. You have a short lead. Use it. Read with your skin.”

He was gone, melting back through the beaded curtain before I could form another question. I was alone again with the painted mountain and the silent, watching teahouse. I looked down at the letter with its mysterious script. Nüshu. A women’s whisper across time. My grandmother’s voice, from beyond the ash, saying: Remember.

I folded the letters away, my hands trembling now. The hook was no longer just in me; it was pulling, drawing me down into a deep, dark water of unrecorded history. I left money on the table and walked out. The alley felt colder. The noises of the market seemed louder, more aggressive. As I turned onto the main thoroughfare, I saw a man in a grey synth-wool jacket leaning against a doorway, staring at a data-slate. He was too clean for this district. He didn’t look up as I passed.

I walked faster, the weight of the letters against my chest feeling less like a secret, and more like a target.

Chapter 2

Chapter 2

The Museum of Fragmented Histories was a sarcophagus of light. Twenty-four floors of curated silence, where the past was chilled, dehumidified, and rendered harmless. My cubicle was on Level 7: “Post-Collapse Social Re-articulation, 2040-2060.” My terminal glowed with the day’s assignments: Cross-reference refugee manifests from the Guangdong Evacuation with subsequent housing allocation logs. Identify discrepancies. Flag anomalies for review.

Anomalies. The word pulsed on the screen. I thought of Leo’s voice in the teahouse. They call them ‘data-corruptions.’

I pulled up the first manifest. A list of names, ID numbers, points of origin. A digital ledger of desperation. My job was to make the numbers align, to smooth out the ragged edges of history into a coherent, state-approved narrative. I usually found a grim solace in the work, in the puzzle of it. Today, the names bled together. My mind was in the yellowed paper of my grandmother’s letter, in the elegant, secret curves of Nüshu.

Read with your skin.

I had spent the night trying. I’d laid the second letter on my kitchen table under a strong light. I’d taken notes, not of meaning, but of form. The script flowed vertically, in columns from right to left. The characters were syllabic, each representing a sound. I’d found scant references in the Museum’s restricted folklore databases—just a footnote: Nüshu (女書): A gendered script, socio-linguistic curio. Suppressed mid-20th century. Considered extinct. No key. No lexicon.

But as I’d traced the shapes with my eyes, something happened. Not understanding, but recognition. A muscle memory. The curve of a particular loop felt like the gesture my grandmother used to make when brushing my hair behind my ear. The sharp terminal of another was like the angle of her shoulder as she bent over her sewing. The language was not in my brain; it was in my bones, a faint, genetic hum. It was maddening.

“Zixin. You’re staring.”

I jumped. Lin stood at the entrance to my cubicle, holding two cups of synthetic coffee. She was my only friend in the Museum, a fellow archivist with a cynicism as polished as the floors. She had a hawk’s face and a laugh that sounded like breaking glass.

“Manifest hell,” I said, minimizing the scan I’d secretly made of the Nüshu letter. “The numbers are swimming.”

“They always swim. They’re drowning.” She handed me a cup. The coffee tasted of chemicals and burnt almonds. “You look like you saw a ghost. More than usual for this place.”

I forced a smile. “Bad sleep.”

“Mmm.” Lin didn’t believe me. Her eyes, sharp behind her hexagonal glasses, scanned my face. “Well, wake up. Section Head wants a word. His office. Ten minutes.”

A cold trickle, unrelated to the terrible coffee, went down my throat. “Khoo? What about?”

“Didn’t say. But he had two Security & Integrity guys with him. Looking very… integral.” She sipped her coffee. “You haven’t been accessing unauthorized poetry again, have you?”

My unauthorized accesses were a minor, open secret between us—my forays into black-market archives of pre-Collapse literature. “Just the usual contraband sonnets.”

“Keep your sonnets to yourself today.” She lowered her voice. “There’s a new directive from the Ministry. ‘Enhanced Coherence Protocols.’ They’re running deep-audit algorithms on all archival activity. Looking for… narrative drift.”

Narrative drift. A beautiful, sterile term for heresy. For remembering the wrong things.

“Thanks, Lin.”

She nodded and glided away.

Section Head Khoo’s office was a glass box overlooking the central atrium. It was meant to symbolize transparency. It felt like a display case. Khoo was a small, precise man who wore tailored suits that seemed to absorb light. He didn’t look up from his slate as I entered.

“Archivist Chen. Close the door.”

The door hissed shut, sealing me in. The two men Lin had mentioned stood against the far wall. They wore the dark grey suits of the Ministry of Memory’s internal branch. Their faces were bland, forgettable. Their presence sucked the oxygen from the room.

“Sit,” Khoo said.

I sat in the cold leather chair.

“Your work on the Guangdong manifests has been satisfactory.” He finally looked up. His eyes were black, polished stones. “Your anomaly flag rate is 3.2% below department average. This indicates either exceptional diligence, or a failure to perceive significant incoherence.”

It was an accusation disguised as data. “I follow the verification protocols, sir.”

“Of course.” He steepled his fingers. “Tell me, Archivist Chen. Do you believe history is a stable entity?”

The question was a trap. “History is the record of human activity. Our work is to ensure that record is accurate and complete.”

“A textbook answer.” A thin smile. “But incomplete. History is not just a record. It is a foundation. The foundation of our social cohesion, our national purpose. Cracks in that foundation, even microscopic ones, can lead to catastrophic structural failure. Do you understand?”

“Yes, sir.”

“Good.” He leaned forward. “We have detected a minor… seismic event. In the old port district. A transaction. A physical artifact, of a pre-Collapse nature, changed hands outside the sanctioned collection channels. Our sensors picked up the paper signature, the chemical decay profile. It was an old letter.”

My blood turned to ice. I kept my face still, the way my grandmother taught me when the police would come to the door asking for neighborhood gossip. Be a stone in a river.

“This is concerning,” Khoo continued, watching me. “Such artifacts are cultural contaminants. They carry unverified narratives, emotional payloads. They can disrupt the calibrated present.” He paused. “The transaction was near your residence. The recipient’s biometric silhouette, captured by street-level sensors, bears a 76% match to your profile.”

Seventy-six percent. Not enough for a direct accusation, but more than enough for suspicion. They had seen me. The man in the grey jacket.

“I was in the port district last night,” I said, my voice surprisingly steady. “I went to the old jade market. To find a clasp for a broken necklace of my mother’s. A personal errand.”

“The jade market.” Khoo noted something on his slate. “And did you purchase a clasp?”

“No. The prices were too high.”

“A pity.” He looked at the two grey men. One gave an almost imperceptible nod. “Well, this is likely a coincidence. But you understand, we must be thorough. In the spirit of the new Enhanced Coherence Protocols, your access privileges are being temporarily elevated.”

This was not what I expected. “Elevated?”

“Yes. You are being assigned to a special project. A deep-audit of a newly acquired, and highly sensitive, collection: The Pang Ho Collection. Diaries, letters, and ephemera from a minor official stationed in the New Territories during the Transition. The material is… messy. Full of personal grievances, unsubstantiated claims. Your task will be to extract the verifiable data—weather reports, supply inventories—and tag the rest for cleansing.” He smiled again. “It is a sign of trust, Zixin. Your sensitivity to pattern is recognized. We believe you can distinguish the foundational data from the sentimental noise.”

It was a test. A beautifully constructed one. They were giving me access to a trove of old letters, to see if my “sickness” would flare up. To see if I would be drawn to the wrong things. And they were isolating me, putting me in a digital quarantine where all my keystrokes, all my eye-movements, would be monitored with intense focus.

“I am honored,” I said.

“The work begins tomorrow. You’ll be moved to a secure lab on Level 15. All other projects are hereby suspended.” He stood, signaling the end of the meeting. “Oh, and Zixin? For the duration of this project, you will surrender your personal data drives and submit to a non-intrusive neural resonance scan. Standard protocol for handling classified historical material. To ensure no… cross-contamination.”

They were going to look inside my head. Not my thoughts, but the electrical patterns, the ghost-imprints of recent memory. They would see the teahouse, Leo, the letter. Maybe not clearly, but they would see the emotional signature, the adrenaline spike of a secret meeting.

“Of course,” I heard myself say.

“Dismissed.”

I walked back to my cubicle, my legs moving on autopilot. The trap had been sprung with elegant, bureaucratic precision. I was to be both the mouse and the microscope. My time had shrunk from a “short lead” to hours.

Lin was waiting, her face grim. “Well?”

“Special project. Pang Ho Collection. Deep-audit.”

She whistled softly. “They’re putting you in the box. That collection is a minefield. Half of it’s written in drunken classical Chinese. The other half is paranoid ramblings.” She studied me. “You’re in trouble, Zixin. What did you do?”

“I went to buy a jade clasp.”

“Bullshit.” She glanced around, then leaned in. “Listen. My cousin works in Sensor Analysis. He said there was a priority flag on a paper-signature in the port district last night. Level 3 alert. They never do that for old paper. What did you pick up?”

I met her eyes. Lin was cynical, but she was not an ally of the Ministry. She hated them for redacting her grandfather’s war record into oblivion. “A letter,” I whispered. “From my grandmother. Posthumously.”

Lin’s face went still. Then, a slow, fierce grin spread across her features. “Oh, you beautiful, stupid woman. You have a real, dead-person letter? And they know?”

“They suspect. They’re giving me access to old letters to see what I do. And they’re scanning me tomorrow.”

The grin vanished. “Then you have until tomorrow morning. What’s in the letter?”

“I don’t know. It’s in a code. A women’s script. Nüshu.”

Lin’s eyes widened. She’d heard of it. Every folklorist had. “You need a key. A Rosetta Stone.”

“There isn’t one. Not in any archive.”

“Then you need a reader.” She chewed her lip, thinking. “There’s… there’s a name. An old professor. She was fired from the University for ‘methodological mysticism.’ She claimed to be studying the affective resonance of pre-modern women’s writing. People said she was trying to reconstruct Nüshu. They made her a laughingstock. She lives in the Old University Quarter, above a pharmacy.” Lin grabbed a scrap of archival scrap paper and scribbled. “Dr. Anya Shen. She might be dead. She might be crazy. But she’s your only shot.”

I took the paper. “Lin… why are you helping me?”

She looked at me, her cynicism gone, replaced by a raw, old anger. “Because they erased my grandfather. They turned a man who fought in the monsoon rains into a statistical entry in a logistical report. Fuck their coherence.” She squeezed my arm. “Now go. Call in sick. Get out of here before they change their minds and scan you today.”

I didn’t need telling twice. I logged out, gathered my things, and walked calmly to the transit hub. I didn’t go home. I took three different trams, looping through the city, watching for grey jackets. Then I headed for the Old University Quarter.

The quarter was a museum of a different kind—a museum of faded intellectual ambition. The buildings were ornate, colonial-era stone, now crumbling, their courtyards overgrown. The pharmacy Lin had mentioned was tucked under a stone archway, its windows filled with dusty jars of dried herbs and archaic medical diagrams. A narrow, dark staircase led up beside it.

I climbed. The air smelled of mildew and camphor. The door at the top was painted a peeling blue. I knocked.

A long silence. Then, a voice, cracked and sharp. “Who is it?”

“My name is Zixin Chen. Lin from the Museum sent me. I need to speak with Dr. Shen. It’s about Nüshu.”

Another silence. Bolts slid back, chains rattled. The door opened a crack, secured by a brass chain. One eye, magnified by a thick lens, peered out. It was a deep, watery brown, swimming with intelligence and suspicion.

“Lin is a fool. And you are a risk.”

“I have a letter,” I said, pulling the second letter from my pocket, holding it up to the crack. “In a variant script. I need to read it.”

The eye focused on the paper. It blinked. The door closed. I heard the chain come off. It opened fully.

Dr. Anya Shen was a tiny woman, swallowed by a oversized cardigan. Her white hair was a wild cloud. She stood in a room that was a tsunami of paper—books piled on the floor, scrolls stacked in corners, folios spilling off every surface. The walls were covered in hand-drawn charts of linguistic trees, phonetic maps.

“Come in. Quickly.” She shut the door behind me and relocked it. “Let me see.”

I handed her the letter. She took it to a cluttered desk under a green-shaded lamp, her movements suddenly swift, birdlike. She put on another pair of glasses and bent over the paper. For a full minute, she said nothing. Then she let out a breath, a long, shuddering sigh.

“Where did you get this?”

“My grandmother. She died ten years ago. It arrived yesterday.”

“This is not just Nüshu,” she whispered, her finger hovering over the script. “This is Hai Nüshu—Sea Nüshu. Used by the women of the coastal trading families. A more fluid, adaptive script. It incorporated foreign loan-sounds, maritime symbols.” She looked up at me, her eyes blazing. “It was the communication network of the Salt-Sister Alliance. Smugglers, information runners, resisters of the Great Homogenization. They moved people, yes, but more importantly, they moved stories. They were the keepers of the unofficial history.”

My grandmother. A smuggler of stories.

“Can you read it?” I asked, my heart pounding.

“Parts. The grammar is elusive. It’s poetic, metaphorical. It’s meant to be felt as much as read.” She traced a column of characters. “This… this is a response. It speaks of a ‘bridge of swallows’ that has collapsed. It mentions a ‘garden where the names of flowers are forgotten.’ It is signed with the lotus-key seal, which indicates it is from the Custodian of the Source.” She looked at me. “Your grandmother?”

“She said she was just the hinge.”

“Then the Custodian is another. This letter is acknowledging a loss. The ‘first bird has flown’—you see that phrase? Here. It means a breach. A secret is loose.” She pointed to another section. “This is a question. ‘Does the soil of the island still hold the shape of the root?’”

The Island of Unfinished Sentences. Leo’s words.

“What does it mean?”

“It is asking if the origin point, the source of their correspondence, is still intact. If the memory is still held in the land itself.” Dr. Shen sat back, removing her glasses. She looked suddenly very old and very tired. “This letter is a fragment of a conversation that has been going on for generations. A conversation conducted in a language designed to be forgotten by the powerful. You have the reply. You need the original message. The one that prompted this.”

“Where is it?”

“The ‘island’ is likely not a real island. In their symbology, an ‘island’ is a safe house, an archive, a hidden repository. ‘Unfinished Sentences’ means it holds stories that were interrupted. Stories without endings.” She handed the letter back to me, her hand trembling slightly. “You must find it. And you must be quick. The fact that you have this… it means the network is active. And if it’s active, the Ministry is hunting it. They have always feared the whispers of women. Men build archives. Women keep secrets. The state cannot control a secret.”

“Will you help me?” The question hung in the paper-cluttered air.

Dr. Shen looked at the chaotic, beautiful mess of her life’s work—a work dismissed as mysticism. She looked at the letter in my hand, a tangible piece of the secret history she had only theorized.

“Yes,” she said, her voice firm. “But not here. This place is watched. Old academics are considered harmless, but they still sweep our data occasionally.” She went to a bookshelf, pulled out a battered atlas. “There is a place. A real place that might be a key. The old fishing village of Tai O. It’s a stilt-house community on the water. It was a known node for the Salt-Sisters. They used the waterways as roads. Find the oldest tea merchant there. One who sells gongfu tea. Ask him for ‘the bitterness of the lost spring.’ If he answers, ‘The spring is in the memory of the mountain,’ he will take you to what you need.”

She wrote the name of the tea merchant and directions on a slip of paper. “Go tonight. Don’t use official transit. Take a water taxi from the Aberdeen side. Pay in cash.”

I took the paper, folding it with the letter. “Thank you, Dr. Shen.”

“Don’t thank me. I am not doing this for you. I am doing it for the sentences. They deserve to be finished.” She ushered me to the door. “And Zixin Chen? Your grandmother was a custodian. That makes you an inheritor. The sickness is your inheritance. Try not to die of it.”

I stepped back into the dim stairwell. The door closed softly behind me, the bolts sliding home. I had a destination. Tai O. A village on the water, a place of stilts and secrets. And I had a deadline: the neural scan tomorrow morning. I had to find the island, find the original letter, and understand what had been breached before the Ministry saw the ghost of it in my mind.

I descended the stairs, emerging into the late afternoon light. The city seemed different now—not a grid of power and control, but a palimpsest, with a older, quieter, more stubborn text bleeding through from below. I turned toward the harbor, toward the water-taxis and the fading light. The first bird had flown. I had to find its nest before the hunters did.

Chapter 3

Chapter 3

The water taxi was a rust-scabbed launch with a single, smoky outboard motor. The pilot was a wizened man with a face like cured leather who accepted my cash without a word and pointed to a plastic bench slick with salt spray. I sat, huddling into my jacket as the engine coughed and we pulled away from the Aberdeen promenade.

We left the galaxy of the city’s lights behind, plunging into the velvet dark of the western waters. The only illumination was the boat’s single running light, casting a frail yellow cone on the churning black water. The air grew colder, sharper, smelling of diesel, fish, and deep, briny decay. Islands loomed as darker patches against the dark sky, some crowned with the skeletal remains of communication towers, others simply silent humps of rock.

My mind was a riot of script and suspicion. The shapes of the Nüshu letter floated behind my eyes. Dr. Shen’s words: A conversation across generations. My grandmother, a custodian, a hinge. And now me, the inheritor of a sickness, racing against a scan that would peel back my cortex and expose my disobedience. I checked the time on an old analog watch I’d dug out of a drawer. 8:47 PM. The scan was scheduled for 9:00 AM. Twelve hours. It felt like twelve minutes.

The pilot cut the engine suddenly, letting the boat drift. In the silence, I could hear the lap of water against hull, the distant cry of a night bird.

“Tai O,” he grunted, nodding toward shore.

I saw no village, only a deeper blackness against the land. Then, as my eyes adjusted, pinpricks of light resolved—not the steady glow of electric bulbs, but the flickering, warm dance of oil lamps and candles. The village was a cluster of stilt-houses perched over the water, connected by a web of narrow, precarious-looking boardwalks. It was a place time had forgotten, or more likely, had chosen to ignore.

I paid the pilot extra to wait. He shrugged, lit a cigarette, and settled in, a silhouette against the starless sky.

The boardwalk groaned under my feet. The smell here was profound: drying squid, fermented shrimp paste, mildew, and the sweet, rotten scent of mangrove mud. Music, tinny and ancient, spilled from a radio somewhere. An old woman mended a net by lantern light, her fingers moving with unconscious speed. She didn’t look up as I passed. I felt transparent, a ghost from the glass city moving through a world that had its own, slower, more stubborn



---

Chapter 4

Chapter 4

The boardwalk groaned under my feet. The smell here was profound: drying squid, fermented shrimp paste, mildew, and the sweet, rotten scent of mangrove mud. Music, tinny and ancient, spilled from a radio somewhere. An old woman mended a net by lantern light, her fingers moving with unconscious speed. She didn’t look up as I passed. I felt transparent, a ghost from the glass city moving through a world that had its own, slower, more stubborn heartbeat.

I was looking for a door with a blue tile. That was all the pilot had given me, after the extra notes had disappeared into his pocket. “Ask for the tile. They’ll know.”

The stilt-houses were packed close, their timbers silvered by salt and years. Doors were shut against the damp night. Some had charms nailed to the lintels—twists of red paper, rusted scissors, bundles of herbs gone to grey dust. Then I saw it, halfway down a side-alley of planks that listed toward the black water: a single, glazed tile of cobalt blue, set into the wood beside a doorway. It was the blue of a twilight sky in a childhood memory, a color that had no business here.

I knocked. The sound was swallowed by the wet air.

The door opened a hand’s width. A face appeared in the crack, backlit by a low, smoky light. It was a man of indeterminate age, his skin the texture of cured leather, his eyes lost in a net of fine lines. He held a paring knife, a half-peeled pear in his other hand.

“I’m looking for…” I began, but he cut me off with a slight jerk of his chin, a gesture that encompassed me, the night, my city-smell.

“You’re late.” His voice was a dry rasp, like reeds rubbing together. “She’s waiting.”

He stepped back, opening the door just enough for me to slip through. The room inside was a cave of shadows and clutter. The air was thick with the smell of kerosene, solder, and ozone—the sharp, clean scent of active electronics. It was a dissonant perfume in the ancient house. Shelves bowed under the weight of components: vacuum tubes glowing like amber insects, tangles of wire, cathode-ray monitors with green, sleeping faces. In the center, hunched over a workbench lit by a gooseneck lamp, was a woman.

She was younger than I expected, perhaps in her forties, her black hair streaked with early silver and tied back in a severe knot. She wore oil-stained coveralls. Her hands, moving inside the guts of an open radio chassis, were slender but sure, the fingers tapping solder with a rhythmic, precise tap-tap-hiss. She did not look up.

“Linh,” the man with the pear said, and vanished through a beaded curtain into a back room.

I stood, the damp from my clothes beginning to steam in the warmer air. The woman—Linh—extracted a burnt-out component, tossed it into a tin can with a clatter, and selected a replacement from a tray. She slotted it home, her movements economical, final.

“You have the letter,” she said, not a question. Her accent was flat, metropolitan, sanded of place.

I reached into my inner pocket, my fingers finding the stiff, yellowed envelope. I laid it on the workbench, beside a oscilloscope’s silent screen. She finally looked up, her eyes flicking from the envelope to my face. They were a dark, unreflective brown, the kind that seemed to absorb light rather than give it back.

“You read it,” she said.

“I… tried. It’s Nüshu. My grandmother—”

“I know who your grandmother was.” She wiped her hands on a rag, leaving grey streaks. “A custodian. A hinge. Now broken.” She picked up the envelope but did not open it. She simply held it, as if weighing its moral mass. “You understand it’s a key. Not a message.”

“A key to what?”

“To the archive. The one they didn’t burn.” She nodded toward a monitor. On it, a slow scroll of text moved—not modern logograms, but the slender, oblique strokes of Nüshu. “The Republic digitized memory. Made it searchable, traceable, sterile. But some memories are knives. They cut the hands that try to hold them. So they were written down, the old way, and scattered. Hidden in plain sight. In recipes. In weaving patterns. In lullabies.”

She turned to a different machine, a bulky thing with a mechanical keyboard. “Your grandmother was part of the network that preserved them. The ‘conversation across generations.’” She spoke the phrase with a twist of her mouth, as if its poetry was a necessary but irritating cover for a blunt truth. “Dr. Shen contacted you because the network is compromised. The sweep is coming. They’re not just scanning for deviant thought, Zixin. They’re data-mining the past, looking for the roots of the sickness they’ve defined. Your grandmother’s archive is one of those roots. And you are a living link to it.”

I felt the walls of the stilt-house press closer. The twelve hours I had left shrank, becoming a noose I could feel tightening. “What do you need from me?”

“Your blood,” Linh said, matter-of-fact. “And your ignorance.”

---

The man with the leathery face returned. His name was Chau. He brought bitter tea in chipped cups and a plate of dried fish. He moved through the clutter like a silent current.

“The letter is encoded,” Linh explained, powering up a scanner that hummed with a pre-digital warmth. “The Nüshu text is a carrier. The real map is in the paper itself. The fiber density, the chemical composition of the ink. It points to a physical location. A server, not on any network. A ghost server.”

She placed the letter under the scanner’s hood. On a secondary screen, a spectral image bloomed—not the characters, but a topographical map of the paper’s landscape, a terrain of peaks and valleys. “But to access it, you need a biological key. A genetic echo. Your grandmother’s line.”

Chau took my hand, his grip dry and firm. He pricked my thumb with a sterile lancet, squeezed a single, fat drop of blood onto a glass slide. No ceremony. Just biology. He inserted the slide into a port on a machine that looked like a hybrid of a centrifuge and an antique radio.

“It will take an hour to sequence and cross-reference,” Linh said. “While it works, you learn.”

The next two hours were a crash course in a hidden world. Linh showed me how to identify a “ghost” in the modern data-streams—a piece of information that existed only as a ripple, a purposeful error in a shipping manifest, a dead hyperlink in a public archive that, when fed through a specific decryption algorithm running on Chau’s analog-dial machines, revealed a coordinate, a name, a fragment of a suppressed poem.

“The future they built is a sieve,” Linh said, her face bathed in the green light of a radar screen repurposed to track data-packets. “They look forward, always forward. Cleaning, optimizing. We look backward. We find the holes they left behind.”

Chau taught me the practicalities. How to use a short-wave radio to catch numbers stations broadcasting strings of digits that were addresses for dead-drop sites. How to spot a friendly courier—a specific knot in a fishnet, a particular arrangement of shells on a windowsill. The small, silent language of resistance written in the grammar of everyday decay.

My mind, still humming with the shapes of Nüshu, began to map this new syntax. It was not unlike the women’s script: a language of necessity, born in the gaps of a dominant power, meant to be seen but not understood by the wrong eyes. I felt a grim exhilaration. This was the ropes. This was the texture of the world my grandmother had inhabited.

The machine chimed, a soft, bell-like tone. Linh read the result on a strip of ticker tape that unspooled from its side. Her face tightened.

“The location is on Lantau. The old Tung Chung fort. But the access protocol… it’s a live trap.”

“A trap?”

“The archive is booby-trapped. Not with explosives. With data. If the wrong genetic signature attempts access, or if the access sequence is incorrect, it triggers a purge protocol. It also sends a pinpoint signal to Republic surveillance. A lighthouse, shining straight to them.” She looked at me. “The sequence is a ritual. It must be performed. Actions, not just codes. Your grandmother was a traditionalist.”

“What actions?”

“The tape doesn’t say. The final piece is stored at the site itself. A final test.” She checked a large, military-style clock on the wall. “You have nine hours until your scan. It is a three-hour journey, if the tides are right. You must leave now.”

I stood, my legs stiff. “You’re not coming?”

“Our work is here. We are the network. We stay in the shadows, or the whole web collapses.” Linh handed me a small, heavy device—a handheld scanner with a monochrome screen. “This will lead you to the fort. It reads environmental data—magnetic anomalies, residual radiation. Follow the silence. The fort is a quiet place in a noisy world.”

Chau pressed a cloth-wrapped bundle into my hands. Inside was a canteen, some rice cakes, and a compact, vicious-looking knife with a handle of water-buffalo horn. “For the dogs,” he grunted. “Not the two-legged kind.”

I stepped back out onto the groaning boardwalk. The night was deeper, colder. The pinpricks of lamplight in the stilt-houses seemed fewer. As I made my way back toward where the pilot waited, I noticed a new sound beneath the lap of water and the distant radio: the low, rhythmic thrum of diesel engines, out on the dark water. Not the single-cylinder putter of fishing boats. Something larger.

---

The pilot was nervous. He pointed a calloused finger toward the mouth of the inlet. Against the slightly less-black sky, I saw the outlines of two vessels, blocky and low. Patrol cutters. Their running lights were off, but I could see the faint cherry glow of cigarettes on deck. They were waiting, silent sentinels blocking the most direct route back to the city.

“They arrived an hour ago,” the pilot whispered, his cigarette trembling. “Asking about a stolen boat. A fugitive from the city. They have biometric scanners.”

Dr. Shen’s warning echoed. They will find you. The sweep was not just a morning appointment; it was a living net, already cast and drawing tight.

“Is there another way?” I asked.

The pilot eyed the bundle in my hands, the new set of my jaw. He spat into the water. “There is a path. Through the mangroves. Then over the spine of the island. It is for smugglers and ghosts. You will get wet. You will get lost.”

“I have a guide,” I said, holding up Linh’s scanner.

He shrugged, a gesture of pure philosophical resignation, and gestured for me to get back in the boat. Instead of heading for the open water, he turned the prow toward a dense, inky wall of vegetation. We nosed into a narrow channel hidden by overhanging roots. The world closed in. Leaves brushed my face, wet and cold. The smell of rot and salt was overwhelming. The boat’s engine, muffled by the dense growth, sounded like a labored heartbeat.

We traveled in this choked twilight for what felt like an age. Finally, the channel shallowed. The pilot killed the engine. “Here. You walk. Follow the water’s edge west until you see the old watchtower. Then head uphill. The fort is on the other side.”

I climbed out into knee-deep, sucking mud. He didn’t wait, already poling his boat back the way we came, disappearing into the labyrinth. I was alone.

The scanner in my hand came to life, its screen casting a pale blue wash over the twisted roots. It showed a simple compass and a fluctuating waveform. Linh’s words: Follow the silence. I began to walk, each step a struggle against the clinging mud. The mangrove was a cathedral of grotesque shapes, the roots like arched ribs, the canopy shutting out the sky. I heard things slither and plop into the water. Once, a pair of red eyes gleamed from a hollow before winking out.

The silence the scanner sought was not an absence of sound, but a specific null in the electromagnetic buzz of the modern world. I was a blind man following a thread of nothing.

After an hour, the mangroves thinned. I scrambled up a rocky slope, my clothes soaked and filthy. From the rise, I could see the patrol cutters, still lurking at the inlet’s mouth. And beyond them, the glittering tumor of the city, my home, waiting to dissect my mind at dawn. The scanner’s waveform flattened into a steady line. I was in a dead zone. I turned inland.

The climb was brutal. The island’s spine was a jagged ridge of volcanic rock, scoured by wind. The scanner was useless here; the silence was total. I had only Chau’s knife and my own failing stamina. I thought of my grandmother walking these same hills, not as a fugitive, but as a custodian. Had she been afraid? Or was she filled with the grim purpose of a scribe, preserving the words that others sought to erase?

Near the summit, I found the watchtower—a crumbled concrete pillar from some forgotten war, strangled by vines. From its base, I saw my destination on the opposite slope: the Tung Chung fort. A star-shaped outline of low, crumbling walls, being slowly digested by the jungle. A place where the past lay in ruins.

As I began my descent, a new sound froze me in my tracks. Not the wind. Not an animal.

A drone. Not the common silver kind, but a matte-black, six-rotor model used for tactical surveillance. It moved with a predatory patience, sweeping back and forth above the fort’s perimeter like a hunting hawk. Its single lens, a dark glass eye, glinted in the faint starlight.

The clear enemy. Not a faceless Republic, but its physical, mechanical proxy. It was here because someone knew. Someone had anticipated the archive’s location. Dr. Shen? A leak in Linh’s network? Or had my own flight painted a target on this place?

I crouched behind a boulder, my heart hammering against my ribs. The drone completed its pass and hovered, facing away from me, toward the sea. This was my test. The first challenge of the ritual. To reach the fort, I had to pass the sentinel.

I remembered Chau’s lesson about environmental silence. The drone was a noisy node. It relied on sensors, on data-link. I looked at Linh’s scanner. In this dead zone, the drone’s signal would be a screaming beacon. I switched modes, searching for its control frequency. The screen fizzed with static, then resolved into a sharp, pulsing band. I couldn’t jam it. But I could perhaps give it a louder signal to listen to.

I took the canteen and the knife. I hurled the canteen in a high arc away from me, to the north. It clattered loudly on the rocks. Instantly, the drone’s head swiveled, its body tilting as it shot toward the sound. In that moment of diverted attention, I ran, low and fast, down the slope and through a breach in the fort’s outer wall.

I collapsed inside, pressing myself against the cold, damp stone. I heard the drone return, its buzz angry now, circling higher, expanding its search pattern. But I was inside the castle keep. For now.

The fort’s courtyard was a bowl of shadows, filled with the skeletons of old barracks and the scent of wet stone and wild ginger. In the center stood a single, squat building with a metal door, surprisingly intact. The server bunker.

This was where the group dynamic would have been tested, had I a group. The loneliness was a physical weight. I had only the legacy of a ghost and the lessons of an hour. I approached the door. There was no keyhole, no terminal. Just a smooth steel plate. Remembering Linh’s words—a ritual—I placed my hand against it. It was icy.

Nothing happened.

Then, a seam of light appeared around the door’s edge, not electric, but a soft, bioluminescent blue. A voice, synthesized yet hauntingly familiar in its cadence, spoke from a hidden grille. It was my grandmother’s voice, reconstructed from some long-lost recording.

“To remember is to disobey,” it said. “The first disobedience is to speak. Speak the first word you were taught to forget.”

My mind went blank, then raced. The first word? A child’s word? A political word? Then I understood. It was not a word from the Republic’s lexicon. It was a word from her lexicon. From the secret language.

I opened my mouth. The Nüshu character for “mother” was a simple, sloping line. Its sound, in the dialect she’d whispered to me as a child, was a soft, sighing exhalation: “Miáng.”

I spoke it to the dark courtyard, to the listening drone above, to the past.

The blue light brightened. The metal door hissed and swung inward on silent hinges. The air that wafted out was cool, dry, and carried the smell of ozone and old paper. Inside, I could see the gentle pulse of server lights, rows and rows of them, like a field of dormant fireflies.

But as I stepped across the threshold, the synthesized voice spoke again, this time with a final, flat tone.

“Genetic signature confirmed. Custodian recognized. Purge protocol suspended. Archive unlocked.”

A new sound joined the hum of the servers: a high-frequency ping, repeating at one-second intervals. A locator beacon. Activated not by a wrong answer, but by the right one. The final trap was not to keep people out. It was to announce that someone had come home.

The drone outside changed its pitch, locking onto the signal. Its buzz became a focused whine, descending rapidly toward the fort.

I was inside the archive. I had found the past. And in doing so, I had lit a flare for the future that hunted me. The race was no longer to my scan. It was to whatever truth lay in these blinking servers, before the black drone filled the doorway with its unblinking eye.



---

Chapter 5

Chapter 5

The beacon’s ping was a metronome in the vault of my skull. Each pulse was a hammer tap on the anvil of the coming storm. I stood frozen in the archive’s throat, the door sealing behind me with a sigh of hydraulics, cutting off the view of the courtyard and the descending whine of the drone. The silence inside was not true silence. It was the dense, humming quiet of a machine holding its breath. The air tasted of static and memory.

Rows of black server stacks receded into the gloom, each one studded with constellations of status lights—green, amber, red. They pulsed in no discernible pattern, a silent, chaotic symphony. In the center of the room, on a simple plinth of unpolished granite, sat a single terminal. Its screen was dark, its surface dustless. A chair waited before it, its leather cracked like a map of a dry river delta.

My grandmother’s voice did not speak again. The trap was sprung. The hunt was called. I had minutes, perhaps less.

I moved to the terminal. My shadow, cast by the server lights, stretched and fractured across the floor. The chair groaned as I sat. The screen remained inert. No prompt, no interface. Just a blank, dark mirror showing the hollows of my own face. I placed my hands on the cool metal of the desk. My right thumb found a depression, a faint, smooth wear-mark in the steel. A place where a hand had rested, day after day, year after year. Her hand.

I pressed my thumb into that ghostly impression.

The screen ignited. Not with the Republic’s stark, white interface, but with a wash of soft, sepia light. Characters bloomed across the display. Not standard logograms. These were the flowing, secret scripts of Nüshu, each character a whispered stitch in a hidden tapestry. They scrolled, a gentle, endless waterfall of women’s words. Diaries. Lamentations. Recipes that were codes. Folk songs that were histories. Poems that were weapons.

A single line stabilized at the center of the cascade, written in the sharp, bamboo-break script of the letter.

“To open the box is to let the bird fly. The bird is the truth. The sky is full of hawks.”

A menu crystallized around it. Directory trees in Nüshu. One label caught my eye, a character cluster meaning “The Unburned Library.” I reached for the controls—a trackball, an antique—and guided the cursor. A click. A new window opened, filled with file names. Dates. Not Republic digital stamps, but lunar calendar notations. My breath hitched. I opened one at random.

It was a scan of a handwritten pamphlet. A treatise on soil reclamation using pre-collapse mycorrhizal fungi. Author: Dr. Anya Petrova. Dated forty years post-Collapse. A name and knowledge purged from the Republic’s sanctioned histories. I opened another. Schematics for a water purification system powered by passive solar, its efficiency triple that of the public filtration units. Another. A genetic registry of heirloom seed stocks, with viability markers.

This wasn’t just a repository of forbidden sentiment. It was a toolbox. A seed vault for a dead world, waiting to be planted.

The high ping in my head seemed to grow louder. I worked faster, my fingers clumsy on the old device. I navigated to a directory simply titled “Custodians.” A list of names appeared, each with a small, scanned photograph. I saw my grandmother, Lin Fên, younger than I ever knew her, her eyes fierce behind round spectacles. I saw others, women and a few men, their faces set in the same determined quiet. At the end of the list was a new entry, just created. My name. Zixin. My citizen-ID photo, lifted from some database, stared blankly back at me. The file was tagged: Custodian – Activated. Archive – Unsealed.

Beneath it was a sub-file. “Legacy Protocol.”

I opened it. A video player window launched. The recording was grainy, monochrome, shot in this very room. My grandmother sat in this chair. She was older than in her custodian photo, her hair fully white, her hands knotted with arthritis, but her spine was straight as a ruler. She looked directly into the camera, into a future she would not see.

“Zixin.” Her real voice, not the reconstruction. It was dry, papery, but utterly clear. “If you are seeing this, you spoke the word. You remembered. And you have triggered the beacon. For that, I am sorry, and I am proud. The apology is for the danger. The pride is for the courage. The archive is now yours. Its contents are not a monument. They are a lever. Use them to pry open the cracks in the world they have built.”

She leaned forward slightly, her gaze intensifying.
“The Republic believes the past is a contagion. They are half-right. It is a contagion of possibility. What they have built is a sterile room. It is clean, it is orderly, and it is dying by inches. This archive is the dirt under the fingernails of history. It is the germ that can spark an immune response. Your task is not to preserve this place. It is to scatter it. To infect the future with the past.”

A schematic flashed on the screen behind her—a network map. “The archive is not only here. It is a ghost in the machine. Fragmented copies, echoes, are stored in data-motes across the city. In public access terminals, in library slag heaps, in the memory cores of decommissioned industrial drones. The key you spoke unlocks them all, remotely. The beacon will bring the Black Sky drones. They will sterilize this location. Let them. The knowledge must flow, or it will drown.”

The schematic zoomed in on one node, pulsing red. The Eastern Municipal Data-Terminus. A public relic, a tourist site for school children to see “how data was managed in the primitive days.”

“The Terminus is the distribution hub. From there, the archive can propagate through the old, forgotten pipes of the city. It will take time to initiate. You must go there. You must input the final key sequence. The sequence is the first poem I taught you. The one about the sparrow and the mountain.”

The memory surfaced, unbidden. A whispered song in the dark, her hand patting my back. A child’s rhyme with a cadence that was a code.

On the video, she sat back. The fierceness left her face, replaced by a profound, weary tenderness. “I kept the world from you to protect you. And then I gave it back to you, knowing it would put a target on your back. There is no forgiveness for such a choice, only necessity. Do not try to save the box. Save the bird. Now run.”

The video ended. The screen returned to the scrolling Nüshu.

The whine outside had become a shriek. It was directly overhead. Metal shrieked against stone as the drone began its cutting work on the fort’s roof. Dust sifted from the ceiling in a fine, grey rain.

I had my instructions. But I was one person, in a stone tomb, with a city between me and the Terminus. The past had given me a weapon, but no army.

Then I remembered the two names scrawled on the back of the letter. Liang. Jiao.
  - The roof groaned. A shower of concrete fragments rattled against the server stacks. The blue bioluminescent strips along the floor flickered. I had one move left before the drone breached the chamber.

My hand went to my pocket, to the cheap, burner citizen-cell Old Man Fai had pressed into my hand with the letter. “For the ghosts,” he’d muttered. I powered it on, its glow a sickly green in the archival gloom. No citizen ID attached. A blank slate. I navigated to the public comms directory, my fingers leaving sweaty smears on the plastic. I typed the name: Jiao. The directory churned. In a city of thirty million, it was a needle in a haystack. But my grandmother did not deal in haystacks.

Only one listing appeared without a district identifier or profession tag. Just a name and a string of numbers. A direct line, unlisted, floating in the system’s blind spot.

I pressed call. It rang once, twice. On the third ring, a connection clicked. No voice. Just the faint sound of breathing and the distant, rhythmic clang of metal on metal—a workshop sound.

“The sparrow flies at dawn,” I said, my voice tight. The first line of the poem.

The clanging stopped. A moment of dead air, thick with calculation. Then a woman’s voice, low and rasped with smoke or grit. “The mountain wears it down.” The correct response. The second line.

“The archive is open,” I said. “The beacon is lit. I need a path to the Eastern Terminus.”

A sharp intake of breath. The sound of a heavy tool being set down. “Location?”

“The old fort. In the Green Zone. The Black Sky is on the roof.”

“Green Zone.” She hissed the words. “You picked a scenic grave. Can you reach the river?”

I looked at the schematic still glowing on the main terminal. The fort’s blueprints showed a maintenance conduit, a relic of the ancient climate systems, leading to a silted-up outflow pipe that emptied into the concrete channel of the old river. “Yes.”

“Be at the river marker G-7 in twenty minutes. Look for the Mudlark. Don’t be late. We won’t be waiting.” The line went dead.

Above me, a circular section of the roof glowed cherry-red. The drone’s cutting beam. Time dissolved. I turned to the terminal, my final act as custodian of this tomb. I navigated to the root command. I typed the Nüshu characters for the sparrow poem, the full sequence. A dialog box appeared: Initiate Distributed Propagation? Y/N.

I selected Y.

Primary Archive – Preparing for Encrypted Dispersion. Estimated time to completion: 07:59:59…

A countdown began. The system would spend the next eight hours breaking itself into a thousand encrypted pieces and pushing them into the designated data-motes. This physical archive would be a hollow shell. The knowledge would be in the wind, waiting for the final key at the Terminus to give it shape.

The red circle on the ceiling began to sag, molten metal dripping in fat, hissing tears. I grabbed the burner cell and ran for the rear wall, where the blueprints indicated the conduit. I found it—a rusted grille set low to the floor. I kicked at it. The rust gave way with a shriek of protest. A damp, foul smell of stagnant water and rot billowed out. I crawled into the darkness just as the cut section of roof collapsed behind me in a thunderclap of shattered stone and the piercing, electronic shriek of the Black Sky drone entering the chamber.
  - The conduit was a tight, slime-coated pipe. I moved through absolute blackness, guided by feel and the fading, tremulous glow of the burner cell. The sound of the drone was muffled, then gone, replaced by the drip of water and the skittering of unseen things. The pipe sloped downward. The air grew colder, thicker. After an eternity of crawling, my hands plunged into icy, sluggish water.

I waded forward, the water rising to my chest. A pinprick of grey light appeared ahead. The outflow pipe, clogged with a cataract of plastic debris and silt. I pushed through a curtain of slimy bags and emerged into the half-light of the city.

I was in the river channel. Not a river of water, but a river of concrete, a vast, canyon-like trench that cut through the city’s underbelly. A trickle of chemical-smelling runoff flowed down its center. High above, the undersides of traffic bridges formed a steel sky. Graffiti covered the walls—not the sanctioned murals of the upper levels, but frantic, layered tags and faded political slogans from dead movements.

Marker G-7 was a rusted maintenance ladder bolted to the wall. I clung to it, shivering, the water swirling around my thighs. I checked the burner cell. No signal down here. No way to know if twenty minutes had passed.

Then I heard it—a low, guttural chugging, echoing off the concrete walls. A shape resolved from the gloom downstream. It was a boat, but like no boat I’d seen. It was cobbled together from scrap metal and fiberglass patches, flat-bottomed and wide. A salvaged electric outboard motor purred at its stern, jury-rigged to a humming power cell. At the helm stood a woman.

She was tall, her frame lean and hard under a stained engineer’s coverall. Her hair was shorn close to the scalp. One side of her face was a web of old, shiny scar tissue that pulled at the corner of her eye—the kiss of a chemical burn or an energy flash. She held the tiller with a casual grip, her eyes scanning the banks. They locked onto me. No greeting. Just a assessing, flinty stare.

This was Jiao.

A second figure moved in the boat’s cabin—a hulking shadow that resolved into a man. He was broad-shouldered, his head shaved, his forearms corded with muscle and etched with the faded blue tattoos of a state labor battalion. Liang. He said nothing, merely threw a coiled rope with a metal hook toward the wall. It clanged against the concrete near my ladder.

“Climb,” Jiao said, her voice the same rasp from the call.

I hauled myself out of the water and scrambled down the rope into the boat. The deck was slick with river grime. Liang pulled the rope in, coiling it with practiced efficiency. His eyes, dark and depthless, flicked over me once, then returned to scanning the channel. He smelled of machine oil and stale sweat.

“The fort?” Jiao asked, already turning the boat downstream, the motor’s chug deepening.

“Breached. The primary archive is forfeit. The dispersion is running.”

A muscle in her scarred cheek twitched. “Then we’re on the clock. The Black Sky will do a sector sweep. They’ll extend the search perimeter to the adjacent zones. Including the river.” She nodded to Liang. He opened a rusted locker and pulled out two items. He tossed one to me—a thick, woolen worker’s jacket, smelling of diesel and another man’s labor. The other was a compact, boxy rifle with a worn stock, which he checked with a series of swift, precise motions.

“You know how to use one of these?” Liang asked, his voice a low rumble. It was the first time he’d spoken.

“No.”

He grunted, not in disapproval, but in acceptance of a logistical fact. “Stay low. Stay behind the cabin. If I tell you to get down, you become part of the deck.”

Jiao guided the Mudlark into the main current of the runoff. We moved through a forgotten city. Above us, the soaring spires and glowing tubes were a distant fantasy. Down here, it was all weeping concrete, moss-fed by leaking pipes, and the cavernous mouths of old flood tunnels. We passed the hulks of ancient filtration plants, their windows blind. Jiao navigated by a lore written in rust and water stains, taking turns into tributary channels that weren’t on any public map.

“How did you know her?” I asked, my voice too loud in the industrial quiet.

Jiao didn’t turn. “She pulled me from a burning factory dormitory. Twenty years ago. Safety protocols had been ‘streamlined.’ The fire suppression systems failed. She was there documenting the violations. She dragged six of us out before the Black Sky cordon locked it down. Taught me that systems that see people as integers will always choose the cheaper equation.”

Liang spoke from his post by the cabin. “She gave me a name. After the labor battalion. They strip you of it. Give you a number. She found my records. Gave me back my name. It’s a small thing. Until you don’t have one.”

They were her legacy, too. Not just words in a server, but people in the dark. A different kind of archive.

We travelled for an hour, the tension a live wire strung between us. The high canyon of the river began to give way to older, brick-lined tunnels. The air grew colder. We were entering the city’s prehistoric guts.

“The Terminus is above us,” Jiao said, cutting the motor to a whisper. We drifted into a wider chamber where several tunnels met. A metal platform, slick with condensation, was bolted to one wall. A ladder led up to a heavy circular hatch. “That service shaft goes up to the sub-basement. Old maintenance access. It should be clear. The hard part is the Terminus itself. It’s a public site during the day. But it’s also a node for the Public Security data-tap. It’s watched.”

“What’s the play?” Liang asked, his eyes on the hatch.

“Distraction and insertion,” Jiao said. She looked at me. “You get to the master terminal. It’s in the central hall, behind the glass. You’ll need physical access. Liang will create a diversion at the main power coupling for the district. Lights out for sixty seconds, maybe ninety. That’s your window. You input the sequence. The archive coalesces and begins its broadcast through the old pipes. Then you get out. We meet back at the Mudlark. If we’re not here in fifteen minutes after the lights come back on, you leave. Understood?”

I nodded, a cold knot in my stomach. Liang was already moving, slinging his rifle and grabbing a heavy canvas bag from the locker. He gave Jiao a curt nod and began scaling the ladder to the hatch with surprising grace for a man of his size.

“Wait,” I said. The word left my mouth before I could stop it. They both looked at me. “This… this dispersion. It will trigger a massive response. They’ll lock down the city. They’ll hunt everyone connected. You’re signing your own warrants.”

Jiao’s scarred face didn’t change. “We’ve been living under a warrant our whole lives, Zixin. The only difference is the stamp.” She gestured to the ladder. “Go. Your window is opening. The bird doesn’t wait for the hawks to finish their nap.”

I climbed. The ladder was cold and slippery. At the top, Liang was already working on the hatch wheel. He leaned his weight into it. With a shriek of protesting metal, it broke free and spun. He pushed the hatch open a crack, peered into the gloom above, then shoved it wide. He hauled himself up and reached a hand down for me.

I took it. His grip was like iron.
  - The sub-basement was a cathedral of dead machines. Hulking, cylindrical data-silos from a century ago stood in rows, their casings cracked, their innards stripped. We picked our way through the graveyard by the light of Liang’s torch. He moved like a shadow, pausing at each intersection, listening. The only sound was the drip of water and the hum of distant, modern infrastructure somewhere far above.

We found a stairwell, its concrete steps worn smooth in the center. Liang pointed up, then held up five fingers. Five floors. He opened his bag and pulled out a block of putty-like material, inserting a small metallic detonator. “Power coupling is on level two. I’ll give you two minutes to get to the main hall. When the lights go out, you move. Don’t think. Just move.”

He didn’t wait for an answer. He melted into the darkness of the second-floor landing.

I climbed, my heart a frantic drum against my ribs. The stairwell opened onto a deserted service corridor. It was clean, lit by sterile LEDs. I could hear the muffured sounds of the public gallery above—the recorded narration of a tour guide, the shuffling of feet. A sign pointed to “Public Viewing Gallery – East Wing.” I went the other way, toward “Central Data Hall – Staff Only.”

The door was locked with a keycard reader. I pressed myself against the wall, despair rising. This was as far as I’d get.

Then, a soft click. The door’s lock light winked from red to green. Jiao’s work, somewhere in the system. I slipped inside.

I was on a metal gantry overlooking the Central Data Hall. It was a vast, circular space, preserved as a museum piece. In the center, under a dome of glass, was the Master Terminal—a monstrous, beautiful artifact of brass and polished walnut, covered in manual switches and glowing vacuum tubes. Around it, on the public side of thick glass barriers, tourists and schoolchildren milled, looking at the dead giant of the past.

Between me and the terminal was fifty feet of open gantry, then a final door leading down to the floor. A single security guard in a crisp uniform lounged by that door, looking bored, scrolling on his citizen-cell.

I checked the time on my burner. Any second.

The lights died.

Not a flicker. A total, plunging extinction of the LEDs. The hall was swallowed by a blackness so complete it felt solid. A beat of stunned silence from the public gallery was followed by a rising wave of confused murmurs and a child’s startled cry.

I was already moving, counting my steps in the dark. Twelve. Fifteen. My shin hit a low railing. I vaulted over it, landed off-balance, and scrambled forward. My hands found the cold metal of the door. I fumbled for the handle, turned it. It was unlocked. Jiao.

I stumbled down a short flight of stairs into the main hall. The emergency lights began to stutter on—weak, battery-powered amber globes that cast long, dancing shadows. They illuminated the guard, who was now on his feet, speaking urgently into his comms. His back was to me.

I ducked behind the massive base of a decommissioned server rack and crab-walked toward the glass dome. The terminal was inside, on a raised dais. The door to the dome was a simple latch. A historic replica, not a real lock.

The guard turned, his torch beam slicing through the gloom. It swept over my hiding place. I held my breath. The beam moved on. He was more concerned with the panicking tourists.

I dashed the last ten feet, yanked the latch, and slipped inside the dome. The air was still and cool. Before me was the Master Terminal. A small, modern interface panel had been grafted discreetly onto its side—the link to the old data-pipes. It was dark, offline.

My hands shook. I recalled the sequence from the fort. The full Nüshu poem. I began to type on the modern keypad, transliterating the characters into the input field.

The sparrow flies at dawn,
The mountain wears it down.
The river asks no pardon,
For the forgotten crown.

I hit enter.

Nothing.

Then, a deep, physical thrum vibrated up through the floor. The antique vacuum tubes on the Master Terminal flickered, then glowed a steady, warm orange. Dials on the brass panels twitched. The great machine was waking up, not as a museum piece, but as a conduit.

On the modern interface, text scrolled.
…Receiving encrypted packets…
…Re-assembly in progress…
…Archive Coalescence: 10%... 25%...

It was working. The ghost was entering the machine.

A shout from behind me. The guard. His torch beam fixed on me through the glass. “You! Stop! Hands where I can see them!”

The coalescence ticked over: 50%...

The guard fumbled with his sidearm. He was young, scared. He raised his weapon. “I said freeze!”

75%...

I didn’t move. My eyes were on the scrolling numbers. The past was flooding into the pipes of the city. My grandmother’s voice echoed in my head. Save the bird.

100%.
Dispersion Protocol Complete. Archive Live.

A new message flashed, urgent and red.
Security Override Detected. Propagation Trace Initiated.

Of course. The final trap within the trap. The Republic couldn’t stop the broadcast, but they could trace its origin point. To this terminal. To me.

The main lights slammed back on with a painful, blinding fury. The door to the hall crashed open. Not just the guard. Three Black Sky operatives in matte-black armor, their visored helmets giving them the look of insects, their weapons raised and tracking. They moved with a terrible, synchronized efficiency.

“On the ground! Now!” The amplified voice was devoid of all humanity.

I was out of time, out of moves. I had scattered the seeds. I had lit the flare. Now the harvesters were here.

I raised my hands slowly. My eyes met the blank visor of the lead operative. Behind him, through the glass, I could see the tourists being herded out, their faces pale with fear and confusion. The old world had just whispered into the new one’s ear, and the new one had flinched.

The operative took a step forward, his weapon unwavering. “Custodian Zixin. You are hereby detained for crimes against the Data Integrity of the Republic. You will come with us.”

This was the expected end. The capture. The silencing.

But my grandmother did not deal in expectations.

As the operative reached for me, the Master Terminal behind me let out a deafening, high-pitched shriek—a sound older than the Republic, the sound of a system pushed beyond its limits. Every vacuum tube flared blindingly white and exploded in a shower of glass and sparks. The antique speakers crackled to life, and a voice boomed through the hall, not a whisper, but a roar. It was a chorus of voices, a hundred, a thousand, layered—the archived voices of the women of Nüshu, of the lost scientists, of the forbidden poets. It spoke a single, looping phrase in a dozen dead languages and dialects, a sonic weapon of pure, dissonant memory.

The Black Sky operatives staggered, clutching their helmets as the feedback screamed through their comms. The guard dropped to his knees. The glass of the dome vibrated, then cracked into a spiderweb.

In that moment of shattered control, a side service door—the one I’d entered through—blew inward off its hinges. A smoke canister rolled into the hall, spewing thick, grey fog.

Through the smoke and the deafening shriek, a figure emerged. Not Liang. Jiao. She held not a rifle, but a heavy industrial cutter, its plasma arc burning blue. She didn’t fire. She swung it in a wide, horizontal arc at the base of the Master Terminal’s connection node. There was a flash, a shower of molten metal, and the terminal’s roar died abruptly, plunging the hall into sudden, ringing silence, broken only by the hiss of the smoke canister.

The trace was physically severed. The archive was gone, loose in the city’s veins.

Jiao’s scarred face was a mask of fury and focus. “The river!” she barked at me, her voice cutting through the haze.

The lead Black Sky operative recovered first, raising his weapon. Jiao moved. She didn’t shoot him. She threw the heavy plasma cutter like a discus. It slammed into a bank of historic, glass-encased relay switches on the far wall. They exploded, sending a cascade of glass and a shower of electrical sparks across the path of the operatives.

I ran, choking on smoke, toward the blown service door. Jiao was right behind me. We crashed back into the stairwell and plunged downward, our footsteps echoing like gunshots. Above, the shouts of the operatives were joined by the rising wail of more sirens, converging on the Terminus from all across the city.

We had shattered the expectation. We had not been captured. We had not been silenced.

We had started a fire.

And now the entire, sterile city was the kindling.



---

Chapter 6

Chapter 6

The river was a myth, a whisper, a vein of forgotten filth beneath the city’s polished skin. We descended not into water, but into the city’s old, wet breath. The stairwell from the Terminus service level ended at a rusted hatch, its wheel stiff with decades of mineral crust. Jiao wrenched it open with a shriek of metal. The smell that rose was not of rain and diesel, but of slow decay and cold stone, of algae and the metallic tang of run-off from a hundred illegal forges. It was the smell of the city’s past, before the spires, before the silent drones. It was the smell of a wound that never closed.

We dropped into a service tunnel, our boots splashing in an inch of oily water. The light from Jiao’s palm-torch cut a frail beam through the absolute dark, catching the gleam of riveted iron walls sweating condensation, the ghostly white threads of mycelium clinging to the seams. The sirens from above were a muffled throb here, a distant headache. Our own panting breaths were louder.

“They won’t follow,” Jiao said, not as reassurance, but as a tactical report. Her voice was flat, emptied of the fury she’d wielded in the dome. “Black Sky protocols. Unmapped subsurface is a contamination risk. They’ll seal the exits and wait.”

“For what?”

“For us to die down here.” She began walking, the beam of her light jerking ahead. “Or for the city maintenance drones to flush the system. Whichever comes first.”

I followed, the shock of the escape hardening into a cold, heavy lump in my gut. We had done it. We had broken the trace. The archive of the Ghost Quarter—my grandmother’s life, the forbidden poets, the shape of a world before the Republic—was now a phantom in the data-streams, a story without a book. It should have felt like victory. It felt like amputation. The terminal’s scream was gone, but a deeper silence had taken its place, ringing in my bones. I had held the root of a memory, and now I held only its echo.
